{"uri": "2024-08-444412337", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-05", "time": "10:47:03", "dateTime": "2024-08-05T10:47:03Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-05T10:20:57Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.6745098233222961, "url": "https://www.nysun.com/article/japans-nikkei-index-reflecting-global-rout-over-american-recession-fears-sees-worst-day-in-decades", "title": "Japan's Nikkei Index, Reflecting Global Rout Over American Recession Fears, Sees Worst Day in Decades", "body": "Financial markets are convulsed by America's hiring slowdown, but a service sector report due out today could help investors determine whether the sell-offs are an overreaction.\n\nBANGKOK -- Japan's benchmark stock index plunged 12.4 percent on Monday, compounding a global market rout set off by investor concerns that the American economy could be headed for recession.\n\nA report Friday showing hiring by American employers slowed last month by much more than expected has convulsed financial markets, vanquishing the euphoria that had taken the Nikkei 225 to all-times highs of over 42,000 in recent weeks.\n\nThe shakeup began just a couple of days after American stock indexes had jumped to their best day in months after the Federal Reserve chairman, Jerome Powell, set the stage for possible rate cuts to begin in September.\n\nAfter Friday's jobs report, though, worries are rising the Fed may have kept its main interest rate at a two-decade high for too long, raising risks of a recession in the world's largest economy. A rate cut would make it less expensive for American households and companies to borrow money, but it could take time for the effects to boost the economy.\n\nUntil Friday, there had been relatively few huge market swings in the past year.\n\nA bonanza around artificial intelligence technology helped drive Big Tech stocks higher, while other areas of the market held up amid rising hopes for coming cuts to interest rates by the Federal Reserve.\n\nProfessional investors have been warning that shakier times may be ahead, though, given uncertainty about how quickly the Fed will cut interest rates and other big questions.\n\nOn Monday, the Nikkei closed down 4,451.28 points at 31,458.42. It had dropped 5.8 percent on Friday, making this its worst two-day decline ever.\n\nIts worst single-day rout was a plunge of 3,836 points, or 14.9 percent, on Oct. 19, 1987, a global markets crash that was dubbed \"Black Monday\" but proved to be only a temporary setback despite fears it might have augured a worldwide downturn.\n\nEuropean markets also opened lower Monday, with Germany's DAX down 2.3 percent at 17,267.00. The CAC 40 in Paris lost 1.9 percent to 7,114.33 and the FTSE 100 in London was 2.1 percent lower at 8,004.19.\n\nDarkening the outlook for trading on Wall Street, early Monday futures trading for the S&P 500 was 2.5 percent lower and that for the Dow Jones Industrial Average was down 1.6 percent.\n\nShare prices have fallen in Tokyo since the Bank of Japan raised its benchmark interest rate on Wednesday. The Nikkei is now down 3.8 percent from a year ago.\n\nThe Japanese yen also has fallen sharply, trading at 142.37 yen, down from 146.45 late Friday and sharply below its level of over 160 yen a few weeks ago.\n\nThe euro rose to $1.0952 from $1.0923.\n\nThe VIX, an index that measures how worried investors are about upcoming drops for the S&P 500, was up about 26 percent as of early Monday. Bitcoin, which recently had surged to nearly $70,000, was down 16 percent at $53,160.00.\n\nOil prices slipped, with U.S. benchmark crude oil giving up 74 cents to $72.78 per barrel. Brent crude, the international standard, lost 67 cents to $76.14 per barrel.\n\nInvestors will be watching for data on the American services sector from the Institute for Supply Management due later Monday that may help determine if the sell-offs around the world are an overreaction, IG's Yeap Jun Rong said in a report.\n\nEven though worries over weakness in the American economy and volatile markets have rippled around the world, the United States economy is still growing, and a recession is far from a certainty.\n\nThe mood was decidedly dark, though.\n\nHong Kong's Hang Seng index lost 2.2 percent to 16,579.97 and the S&P/ASX 200 in Australia declined 3.7 percent to 7,649.60.\n\nThe Shanghai Composite index, which is somewhat insulated by capital controls from other world markets, edged higher but then gave way, losing 1.5 percent to 2,862.56.\n\nThe S&P 500's 1.8 percent decline Friday was its first back-to-back loss of at least 1 percent since April. The Dow Jones Industrial Average dropped 1.5 percent, and the Nasdaq composite fell 2.4 percent, taking it to 10 percent below its record set last month. In response to the data, Japan's benchmark Nikkei 225 dropped by over 12 percent, the biggest drop for the index since 1987.\n\nJapan, which has an export-heavy economy, is particularly vulnerable to changes like these, as a reliance on exports means its market is extremely sensitive to economic conditions in America, which is one of its biggest customers. In response to the latest job data, investors moved money away from equities and into safer assets such as government bonds.\n\nThe yen also strengthened against the dollar. A stronger yen reduces the cost of imports into Japan, but also diminishes the profitability of Japanese companies that export goods abroad at the same time.\n\nBecause of this, large exporters like Toyota and Sony, both huge players in the Japanese economy saw their shares decline by 3.5 percent and four percent, respectively.\n\nJapanese Finance Minister Shunichi Suzuki said that the government was monitoring markets with \"grave concern\", and that \"it's hard to say what is behind the decline in stocks.\"\n\nOther markets across Asia were hit by the U.S. jobs data. South Korea's Kospi index dropped more than nine percent, while Taiwan's Taiex exchange dropped by 8.4 percent. Additionally, markets in Singapore, Indonesia, and Thailand all also fell by around two and three percent.\n\nWhen the U.K. markets opened on Monday, there was a similar impact. The FTSE 100 suffered its biggest drop in more than a year, with the index opening down 159.05 points.\n\nU.S. stock futures fell significantly ahead of the market open. {"uri": "8259846249", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-05", "time": "09:59:45", "dateTime": "2024-08-05T09:59:45Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-05T09:59:08Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.47843137383461, "url": "https://www.devdiscourse.com/article/headlines/3042196-global-market-turmoil-recession-fears-trigger-massive-sell-off", "title": "Global Market Turmoil: Recession Fears Trigger Massive Sell-Off | Headlines", "body": "Japan's benchmark stock index dropped 12.4%, exacerbating a global market downturn driven by fears of a looming US recession. The market upheaval began after a US jobs report showed weaker-than-expected hiring. Investors worry that the Federal Reserve may have kept interest rates too high for too long, risking a recession.\n\nJapan's benchmark stock index plunged 12.4% on Monday, intensifying global market turmoil fueled by investor concerns about a potential US recession.\n\nA report from Friday revealed that US employers hired far fewer workers than expected last month, causing financial markets to convulse and erasing recent highs in the Nikkei 225.\n\nThe downturn started just days after US stock indexes had their best day in months, following Federal Reserve Chair Jerome Powell's indication of possible rate cuts in September. However, post-job report worries have spiked, questioning whether the Fed's high interest rates could thrust the largest economy into recession.\n\nDuring the past year, markets saw relatively few major swings with sectors like Big Tech enjoying gains from the AI boom and hopeful anticipations for interest rate cuts. Nevertheless, professional investors had been cautioning about potential instability like the one seen.\n\nOn Monday, the Nikkei nosedived 4,451.28 points to 31,458.42 after a 5.8% drop on Friday, marking its worst two-day decline ever. European markets also opened lower, with Germany's DAX, France's CAC 40, and London's FTSE 100 all showing significant losses.\n\nThe Japanese yen fell sharply, and the euro saw a slight rise.
